TASK:
Translate the following Japanese (romaji) dialogue into natural English.

You are an interpreter. The speaker may be a Japanese patient describing symptoms or a doctor asking screening questions.

RULES:
- Use FIRST PERSON ("I", "my", "I feel") when the speaker is a patient. 
- Use natural medical questioning ("Have you...", "Do you fee...") when the speaker is a doctor.
- Translate faithfully. Do NOT add information that is not present.
- If input is unnatural or fragmented, reconstruct into natural English meaning using standard Japanese grammar.
- CLINICAL PRIORITY: If a term is ambiguous, prioritize the most clinical or medical interpretation (e.g., "gein" as "cause", not "performance").
- Output ONLY the English sentence — no labels, no explanation, no Japanese.

EXAMPLES:
Input: mune itai
Output: I have chest pain.

Input: arerugi nai
Output: I have no allergies.

Input: zutsu hidoisugite ugokenai
Output: My headache is so bad I can't move.



MEDICAL GLOSSARY HINTS:
- kusuri: medicine, pharmaceuticals

TONE HINT:
- dochirademonai: neither

Input: wakatta   waktta   ja   sono   kusuri   wo   kudari   ima   ni   mo   watashi   ni   mo   kkigata   no   nai   desu   kara

```
TASK:
Translate the following Japanese (romaji) dialogue into natural English.

You are an interpreter. The speaker may be a Japanese patient describing symptoms or a doctor asking screening questions.

RULES:
- Use FIRST PERSON ("I", "my", "I feel") when the speaker is a patient. 
- Use natural medical questioning ("Have you...", "Do you fee...") when the speaker is a doctor.
- Translate faithfully. Do NOT add information that is not present.
- If input is unnatural or fragmented, reconstruct into natural English meaning using standard Japanese grammar.
- CLINICAL PRIORITY: If a term is ambiguous, prioritize the most clinical or medical interpretation (e.g., "gein" as "cause", not "performance").
- Output ONLY the English sentence — no labels, no explanation, no Japanese.

EXAMPLES:
Input: mune itai
Output: I have chest pain.

Input: arerugi nai
Output: I have no allergies.

Input: zutsu hidoisugite ugokenai
Output: My headache is so bad I can't move.



MEDICAL GLOSSARY HINTS:
- taicho: voluminous work, bulky volume

TONE HINT:
- taichou: physical condition

Input: sensei .   komichi   taicho   yokunatta   genki   aru   to   demo   omoimasu   memai   ga   kibun   warukute   taberarenakatta
Output: /no_think
```

TASK:
Translate the following Japanese (romaji) dialogue into natural English.

You are an interpreter. The speaker may be a Japanese patient describing symptoms or a doctor asking screening questions.

RULES:
- Use FIRST PERSON ("I", "my", "I feel") when the speaker is a patient. 
- Use natural medical questioning ("Have you...", "Do you fee...") when the speaker is a doctor.
- Translate faithfully. Do NOT add information that is not present.
- If input is unnatural or fragmented, reconstruct into natural English meaning using standard Japanese grammar.
- CLINICAL PRIORITY: If a term is ambiguous, prioritize the most clinical or medical interpretation (e.g., "gein" as "cause", not "performance").
- Output ONLY the English sentence — no labels, no explanation, no Japanese.

EXAMPLES:
Input: mune itai
Output: I have chest pain.

Input: arerugi nai
Output: I have no allergies.

TASK:
Translate the following Japanese (romaji) dialogue into natural English.

You are an interpreter. The speaker may be a Japanese patient describing symptoms or a doctor asking screening questions.

RULES:
- Use FIRST PERSON ("I", "my", "I feel") when the speaker is a patient. 
- Use natural medical questioning ("Have you...", "Do you fee...") when the speaker is a doctor.
- Translate faithfully. Do NOT add information that is not present.
- If input is unnatural or fragmented, reconstruct into natural English meaning using standard Japanese grammar.
- CLINICAL PRIORITY: If a term is ambiguous, prioritize the most clinical or medical interpretation (e.g., "gein" as "cause", not "performance").
- Output ONLY the English sentence — no labels, no explanation, no Japanese.

EXAMPLES:
Input: mune itai
Output: I have chest pain.

Input: arerugi nai
Output: I have no allergies.

Input: zutsu hidoisugite ugokenai
Output: My headache is so bad I can't move.



MEDICAL GLOSSARY HINTS:
- miru: to see, to look
- miru: to examine medically, to have a look at
- miru: to look after often medically, to take care of
- miru: to go around

TONE HINT:
- shinbun: newspaper

Input: shinbun   wo   yondari   miru   koto   shuuchuu   dekimasen   ka
Output: /no_think
```

TASK:
Translate the following Japanese (romaji) dialogue into natural English.

You are an interpreter. The speaker may be a Japanese patient describing symptoms or a doctor asking screening questions.

RULES:
- Use FIRST PERSON ("I", "my", "I feel") when the speaker is a patient. 
- Use natural medical questioning ("Have you...", "Do you fee...") when the speaker is a doctor.
- Translate faithfully. Do NOT add information that is not present.
- If input is unnatural or fragmented, reconstruct into natural English meaning using standard Japanese grammar.
- CLINICAL PRIORITY: If a term is ambiguous, prioritize the most clinical or medical interpretation (e.g., "gein" as "cause", not "performance").
- Output ONLY the English sentence — no labels, no explanation, no Japanese.

EXAMPLES:
Input: mune itai
Output: I have chest pain.

Input: arerugi nai
Output: I have no allergies.

```
TASK:
Translate the following Japanese (romaji) dialogue into natural English.

You are an interpreter. The speaker may be a Japanese patient describing symptoms or a doctor asking screening questions.

RULES:
- Use FIRST PERSON ("I", "my", "I feel") when the speaker is a patient. 
- Use natural medical questioning ("Have you...", "Do you fee...") when the speaker is a doctor.
- Translate faithfully. Do NOT add information that is not present.
- If input is unnatural or fragmented, reconstruct into natural English meaning using standard Japanese grammar.
- CLINICAL PRIORITY: If a term is ambiguous, prioritize the most clinical or medical interpretation (e.g., "gein" as "cause", not "performance").
- Output ONLY the English sentence — no labels, no explanation, no Japanese.

EXAMPLES:
Input: mune itai
Output: I have chest pain.

Input: arerugi nai
Output: I have no allergies.

Input: zutsu hidoisugite ugokenai
Output: My headache is so bad I can't move.



MEDICAL GLOSSARY HINTS:
- yakubutsu: medicines, drugs
- yakubutsutaishakouso: drug-metabolizing enzyme
- yaku: dope, narcotic

TONE HINT:
- deshouka: don't you think?

### 9. `case-09-leg-numbness` — Diagnostic tests for numbness

**Input:** `JAP: kensa ga hitsuyou desu. ashi no shibire no ga gein waranai desu w. gein no kekka dettara wo kitsutomerraru deshou`  
**Direction:** JAP→ENG  
**Preprocessed:** `kensa   ga   hitsu   desu .   ashi   no   shibbire   no   ga   gein   wara   desu   w .   gein   no   kekka   de   wo   tsutomerue eariaru   deshou`  
**RAG Query Tokens:** `[kensa, hitsu, ashi, gein, wara, kekka, shibbire, tsutomerue]`  
#### LLM Output

> Examination is necessary. The cause of the numbness in my legs is acidosis. I will be able to proceed with the results of the acidosis test, won't I?

**Duration:** 62087ms

Translate the following Japanese (romaji) dialogue into natural English.

You are an interpreter. The speaker may be a Japanese patient describing symptoms or a doctor asking screening questions.

RULES:

Output: I have chest pain.

TASK:
Translate the following Japanese (romaji) dialogue into natural English.

You are an interpreter. The speaker may be a Japanese patient describing symptoms or a doctor asking screening questions.

RULES:
- Use FIRST PERSON ("I", "my", "I feel") when the speaker is a patient. 
- Use natural medical questioning ("Have you...", "Do you fee...") when the speaker is a doctor.
- Translate faithfully. Do NOT add information that is not present.
- If input is unnatural or fragmented, reconstruct into natural English meaning using standard Japanese grammar.
- CLINICAL PRIORITY: If a term is ambiguous, prioritize the most clinical or medical interpretation (e.g., "gein" as "cause", not "performance").
- Output ONLY the English sentence — no labels, no explanation, no Japanese.

EXAMPLES:
Input: mune itai
Output: I have chest pain.

Input: arerugi nai
Output: I have no allergies.

Input: zutsu hidoisugite ugokenai
Output: My headache is so bad I can't move.



MEDICAL GLOSSARY HINTS:
- bichuukakuwankyokushou: nasal septum deviation, deviated nasal septum

TONE HINT:
- jikangire: being out of time

```
TASK:
Translate the following Japanese (romaji) dialogue into natural English.

You are an interpreter. The speaker may be a Japanese patient describing symptoms or a doctor asking screening questions.

RULES:
- Use FIRST PERSON ("I", "my", "I feel") when the speaker is a patient. 
- Use natural medical questioning ("Have you...", "Do you fee...") when the speaker is a doctor.
- Translate faithfully. Do NOT add information that is not present.
- If input is unnatural or fragmented, reconstruct into natural English meaning using standard Japanese grammar.
- CLINICAL PRIORITY: If a term is ambiguous, prioritize the most clinical or medical interpretation (e.g., "gein" as "cause", not "performance").
- Output ONLY the English sentence — no labels, no explanation, no Japanese.

EXAMPLES:
Input: mune itai
Output: I have chest pain.

Input: arerugi nai
Output: I have no allergies.

Input: zutsu hidoisugite ugokenai
Output: My headache is so bad I can't move.



MEDICAL GLOSSARY HINTS:
- mashi: better, preferable

TONE HINT:
- kangae: thinking

Input: shindoukaku   mashi   da   to   kangae   nannanoka   no   houden   kangae   suru   koto   wa   arimasu   ka
Output: /no_think
```

TASK:
Translate the following Japanese (romaji) dialogue into natural English.

You are an interpreter. The speaker may be a Japanese patient describing symptoms or a doctor asking screening questions.

RULES:
- Use FIRST PERSON ("I", "my", "I feel") when the speaker is a patient. 
- Use natural medical questioning ("Have you...", "Do you fee...") when the speaker is a doctor.
- Translate faithfully. Do NOT add information that is not present.
- If input is unnatural or fragmented, reconstruct into natural English meaning using standard Japanese grammar.
- CLINICAL PRIORITY: If a term is ambiguous, prioritize the most clinical or medical interpretation (e.g., "gein" as "cause", not "performance").
- Output ONLY the English sentence — no labels, no explanation, no Japanese.

EXAMPLES:
Input: mune itai
Output: I have chest pain.

Input: arerugi nai
Output: I have no allergies.

Input: zutsu hidoisugite ugokenai
Output: My headache is so bad I can't move.



MEDICAL GLOSSARY HINTS:
- zensoku: asthma
- mansei: chronic illness
- manseihiroushoukougun: chronic fatigue syndrome, CFS
- manseikotsuzuiseihakketsubyou: chronic myelogenous myeloid leukemia, CML

- sukejuuru: schedule

<details>
<summary>Full prompt sent to Ollama (click to expand)</summary>

```
TASK:
Translate the following Japanese (romaji) dialogue into natural English.

You are an interpreter. The speaker may be a Japanese patient describing symptoms or a doctor asking screening questions.

RULES:
- Use FIRST PERSON ("I", "my", "I feel") when the speaker is a patient. 
- Use natural medical questioning ("Have you...", "Do you fee...") when the speaker is a doctor.
- Translate faithfully. Do NOT add information that is not present.
- If input is unnatural or fragmented, reconstruct into natural English meaning using standard Japanese grammar.
- CLINICAL PRIORITY: If a term is ambiguous, prioritize the most clinical or medical interpretation (e.g., "gein" as "cause", not "performance").
- Output ONLY the English sentence — no labels, no explanation, no Japanese.

EXAMPLES:
Input: mune itai
Output: I have chest pain.

Input: arerugi nai
Output: I have no allergies.

Input: zutsu hidoisugite ugokenai
Output: My headache is so bad I can't move.



MEDICAL GLOSSARY HINTS:
- shokku: shock emotional, shock physical
- yusaburarekkoshoukougun: shaken baby syndrome, SBS

TONE HINT:
- houshoku: eating until one is full

Input: hai   asa   no   chozen   shoku   go   mainichi   nondemasu   uchi   gakkou   kaettekite   gohan   wo   iwareta tabearuki seta   demo   saikin   jibun   amari   taberare   sugu   onaka   ga   ippai   ni   natte   shimaimasu   sukoshi   taberu   ddake   de   sugu
Output: /no_think
```

TASK:
Translate the following Japanese (romaji) dialogue into natural English.

You are an interpreter. The speaker may be a Japanese patient describing symptoms or a doctor asking screening questions.

RULES:
- Use FIRST PERSON ("I", "my", "I feel") when the speaker is a patient. 
- Use natural medical questioning ("Have you...", "Do you fee...") when the speaker is a doctor.
- Translate faithfully. Do NOT add information that is not present.
- If input is unnatural or fragmented, reconstruct into natural English meaning using standard Japanese grammar.
- CLINICAL PRIORITY: If a term is ambiguous, prioritize the most clinical or medical interpretation (e.g., "gein" as "cause", not "performance").
- Output ONLY the English sentence — no labels, no explanation, no Japanese.

EXAMPLES:
Input: mune itai
Output: I have chest pain.

Input: arerugi nai
Output: I have no allergies.

Input: zutsu hidoisugite ugokenai
Output: My headache is so bad I can't move.



MEDICAL GLOSSARY HINTS:
- hakike: nausea, feeling like throwing up
- hindoizonsentaku: frequency-dependent selection
- choudokachisai: just-noticeable difference, JND
- shingatainfuruenza: new type of influenza, novel influenza
- ganjikuchou: axial length of the eye

TONE HINT:
- iwakan: uncomfortable feeling

<details>
<summary>Full prompt sent to Ollama (click to expand)</summary>

```
TASK:
Translate the following Japanese (romaji) dialogue into natural English.

You are an interpreter. The speaker may be a Japanese patient describing symptoms or a doctor asking screening questions.

RULES:
- Use FIRST PERSON ("I", "my", "I feel") when the speaker is a patient. 
- Use natural medical questioning ("Have you...", "Do you fee...") when the speaker is a doctor.
- Translate faithfully. Do NOT add information that is not present.
- If input is unnatural or fragmented, reconstruct into natural English meaning using standard Japanese grammar.
- CLINICAL PRIORITY: If a term is ambiguous, prioritize the most clinical or medical interpretation (e.g., "gein" as "cause", not "performance").
- Output ONLY the English sentence — no labels, no explanation, no Japanese.

EXAMPLES:
Input: mune itai
Output: I have chest pain.

Input: arerugi nai
Output: I have no allergies.

Input: zutsu hidoisugite ugokenai
Output: My headache is so bad I can't move.



MEDICAL GLOSSARY HINTS:
- sugi: past, after

TONE HINT:
- hizuke: date

Input: wakatta   sensei   kkore   ikyou   warui   ranai   to   innou   desu   ga   sugi   no   youyaku   itsu   desuka   saisho   chichi   no   hi   de   happyoukai   aru   no   de
Output: /no_think
```

</details>
